  President . – The next item is the recommendation for second reading (A4‐0320/97), on behalf of the Committee on Culture, Youth, Education and the Media, on the common position established by the Council (C4‐0481/97‐97/0103(COD)) with a view to the adoption of a European Parliament and Council Decision amending Decision No 819/95/EC establishing the Community action programme Socrates (Rapporteur: Mrs Pack).

The rapporteur, Mrs Pack, has the floor.

  Pack (PE), rapporteur. – (DE) Mr President, ladies and gentlemen, at this late hour I will just make a few comments. There are indisputable facts about Socrates – not the late lamented, but the programme.

First, this Socrates programme, with its elements Erasmus and Komenius, is the success story of the European Union, as far as adult and language training are concerned. What needs to be done, what has to be strengthened or improved? Nothing, actually, except that expenditure of money is associated with it.

Secondly, education and training are the important pillars of all efforts for the future that we can undertake today. In Europe our capital lies in the best trained young people of all classes, in other words irrespective of their family origins.

Thirdly, xenophobia can only be reduced and hatred of foreigners prevented by mobility and living with one another in Europe. The Socrates programme in all its parts is the instrument for many young people to achieve a European capability. I am sure – one can read that also in the Council"s joint declaration – that this is common ground. The conclusions in the joint declaration are, however, curiously illogical. Knowing that the programme is a good one; that it has gained acceptance; that young people want to use this programme to invest in European added value for the rest of their lives; the Council takes no cognisance of it.

Today I received by coincidence a letter from a young student who said he wants to take part in Erasmus, is taking part and receives DM 100 per month – ECU 50 for his mobility. He is sorry, but he cannot do it. We first need more money, therefore, in order to meet the desires we have awakened.

Secondly, the Council must give solid financing to its own promises. I want the central and eastern European states and all associated countries also to be able to participate in the Socrates programme. Today I have to ask myself, however, if the Council really wants that too. We want it. The Council owes us proof. A miserable sum of ECU 23 million for 1998 and 1999. That means the Council allows us ECU 12.5 million for next year. How generous! Particularly when we needed over ECU 80 million just for 1997 in order to complete but one part of Erasmus. Parliament is very moderate. Really. In view of the stretched budgetary situation in all Member States we really do not want to be immoderate. We are not quite so moderate, Commissioner Cresson, as you will probably have to be today, unfortunately, for reasons of collegial responsibility. We are not quite so moderate. We ask for ECU 50 million for the next budgetary year – and it is in tomorrow"s budget and we shall vote for it. That is still much less than we planned for in 1994, at a time when neither Sweden, Finland nor Austria was a member, and when there were no associated nations. We still remain today below what we previously planned for the whole programme, what we estimated for the whole duration for 12 countries, namely ECU 1, 000 million. We remain under this today. One cannot be more moderate. We do understand the budgetary situation. I believe we have no option but to appeal to the Council. I hope that the Luxembourg Presidency of the Council really helps us to get over this in the next few weeks. We appeal to the Council to do everything in its power not miss this investment in the future of our children. What we miss today cannot be recovered tomorrow. It is the best possible investment in a good future for the whole of Europe!

  Morgan (PSE). – Mr President, thank you for coming to listen to us debate Socrates again. I hope this will be the final time we are congregated to discuss this issue because we hope we will have a result following conciliation. But it is important that we appreciate the importance of the Socrates programme in its attempts to cement the links between the young people of Europe. It is a programme that has served to combat racism and xenophobia. But if it is to continue its good work, it needs to be properly funded.

If we take the example of the Erasmus student grants, the maximum that people are allowed to get is around ECU 5, 000. If every eligible student were to receive funding, there would only be enough for about ECU 750 per person. This is totally inadequate for those not of independent means. But it is a vital way of ensuring that links between the people of Europe continue.

Parliament has consistently shown its commitment to this programme by supporting a ECU 100 million increase in its funding over the next two years of the programme. The Commission has come up with ECU 50 million. We understand that you have collegiate responsibilities. Of course we then have the big battle with the Council, which has come up with the paltry sum of ECU 25 million. It is important that the Council puts its money where its mouth is with this vital programme. It is important that our children are our future. We understand this, but it is important that our rhetoric is backed up by our commitment and resources.

The programme is also being extended to the ten associated countries of central and eastern Europe. The budgetary effects of this expansion have to be considered. We originally ordered a set meal for our family of 12 people. We then invited 12 more people to the table but I am afraid we did not order any extra food. What we need is more resources for the caterer to ensure that none of our family or our guests leaves our educational table unfed or hungry. Our citizens have an educational and a cultural appetite. We must try to make sure those appetites are satisfied.

  Rübig (PPE). – (DE) Mr President, Madam Commissioner, training is one of the most important instruments available to us for ensuring future employment. There are three main divisions in economic policy: research and development, infrastructure and training. I think that we in Europe have done a lot for training. We have had the Year of Learning for Life, we have Socrates, we have Leonardo, we have Youth for Europe, we have the Green Paper on Training and Continuation Training. I believe it is necessary to do a lot more in this direction in future. Quite simply, it is important for us to obtain people for the professions, for the exercise of the professions, who have the ability to make quantitative and qualitative decisions, who are good at time management, and who involve themselves and try to produce results accordingly.

Socrates in particular is in a position, because of its cross‐border activities, to motivate our young people to become active in this area and to make themselves available to act as models for other young people. Our purpose in training is to see that young people gain experience. In the past it has always been taken for granted that one gained knowledge and then was able to pass it on. But it is also our purpose for them to gain social knowledge, that is, to talk to citizens of other countries, to learn about conditions there, and to bring positive aspects of other countries back home with them.

Finally, there is self confidence, that is, how one appears to others, how one presents oneself. I think that here we can learn a great deal from other countries. I am very pleased that the programme is now open to all 15 states in Europe, including those countries who want to join. I believe that particularly with training we can point the way Europe is going, and I think it is necessary for that reason to invest in this programme, and to make efforts in future to integrate the new Member States into the training programme, passing on our knowledge to them.

If Parliament makes ECU 150 million available for the Luxembourg Summit, I think it is up to the Council and the Commission to give some thought to how this money should best be spent. And I believe that in the training programmes we have the best starting point for tackling employment: for making an employment programme available in future to young people who are currently out of work. Our foremost objective should be integration into work.

  Ryynänen (ELDR). – (FI) Mr President, Madam Commissioner, we in the Union"s decision making body are concerned for that reason about people"s indifference or active dislike of the Union. It is hard for ordinary people to see the positive work of the EU in everyday life. However, it is hoped the Union can promote useful projects to achieve that extra credibility for Europe, and increase crucial understanding and confidence. In fact, this should obviously be the clear aim of international harmonization.

How is the Commission and Council of Ministers going to respond, then, to the challenge when it debates the Socrates programme? The common attitude of the Council, the result of which the extra funding required by Parliament is rejected, is totally incomprehensible in the light of the issue. How can we bring the Union to the people if we are watering down a programme that people in each member country see as important, useful and effective? This Parliament, which represents those people, cannot go along with this. The Socrates programme has already achieved much to improve quality in training in Europe, and it is very important that we carry on with the work to do with the development of further training for teachers and language learning.

Aside from current Member States, the Socrates programme has much to offer applicant countries in Central and eastern Europe. The reciprocal movement must be ensured, and the ECU 100 million claimed by Parliament is the absolute minimum for the programme to be viable and credible. We can"t afford to allow such an excellent and vital programme to wither away into just a kind of elitist programme with dwindling aid and diminishing aided project numbers. The level of aid must be maintained to enable all students to participate in the exchange programme. The situation is particularly awkward in colleges, which do not have the funds to support it.

I sincerely hope that in future arbitration we will be successful in salvaging the EU training policy flag‐ship, Socrates, and that in the future more young people will have the chance of an internationalised and interactive education, regardless of background or origin. I wish Mrs Pack every success in her splendid and purposeful work.

  Elchlepp (PSE). – (DE) Mr President, ladies and gentlemen, I should like to support most energetically the criticism expressed here. The financing of Socrates is indeed scandalous. There are few activities of the European Community in which the personal sense of an increase in European value, the experience of increased value, can be so appreciated, as in training and exchange programmes, and they have been really successful.

No one can deny that. These training programmes also make an increasing contribution to employment policy for young people in Europe, who by learning foreign languages, new legal systems, new mentalities in other countries, gain invaluable knowledge and skills. From these they produce for themselves new opportunities on the European employment market. Yet I am forced to say that precisely here, where Europe can make some of its credibility visible, the Council is proving to be a Scrooge.

They are being utterly insensitive to what the rump of Europe actually perceives: communication and understanding as the basis for integration. The commitment of the German Government and others to the open‐mindedness, work and life of Europe loses its credibility against this background. I should also like to say a quick word about the sociopolitical aspect. It is not just that the payments are so low – and that is itself scandalous – but that this has led to real selection of participants. Anyone today who can afford it goes abroad to study in any case, and if the finances of Erasmus, for example, are not increased, socially underprivileged young people will be unable to go in future years. Without additional finance we shall not be able to improve on the current participation by barely 1 % of young people on professional training courses.

One always finds oneself in a dilemma in one"s constituency. Should one advertise the programmes in schools and workplaces to which one is invited, or should one show a sense of responsibility and keep silent about them, because the ratio of applicants to means is growing ever shorter?

I should also like to comment on making the schemes available to central and eastern Europe. The same heads of government who promise their states EU membership put financial hurdles in the way of a meeting of peoples through the youth exchange scheme. What is an increase of 25 million on a total budget of 860 million, when the countries who are to profit from Socrates in the future amount to about a third of the population of the present EU?

Just continue in this way, one would just say if one were cynical, and the credibility of this EU will sink still further in the eyes of the population. All the glossy brochures in the world will not help here. Reality speaks a different language, and with this financial arrangement we shall not inspire young people for Europe.

  Cresson , Member of the Commission – (FR) Mr President, ladies and gentlemen, first of all I would like to thank Parliament, in particular the Committee on Culture, Youth, Education and the Media for the commitment and determination it has shown in reaching a substantial increase in the budgetary funding of the Socrates programme.

I would like to thank in particular your rapporteur, Mrs Pack, for her work on behalf of this programme which, we all agree, is of prime importance for the future of Europe. It is of proven interest. More than ever the development of our citizens, the fight against social exclusion, the creation of jobs, depend in fact on our investment in education and development throughout life. It is thanks to Socrates and other programmes like Leonardo da Vinci, Youth for Europe and, in the future, European Voluntary Service, that we shall move progressively to a Europe of knowledge and that our fellow citizens" expectations in this field will be realized. But there are many other reasons why Socrates should receive the necessary funding.

First of all because by enabling each year about a hundred million persons to participate actively in European cooperation it contributes to bringing the Union closer to its citizens. This is a role of absolutely prime importance in particular in this phase of ratifying the Treaty of Amsterdam. Then, and you were right to draw attention to this aspect during your first reading of the dossier, it constitutes an important point of support for the enlargement of the Union to include countries of central and eastern Europe.

However, the Council has not supported us in our proposal for a significant increase in the initial financial framework of the programme. The ECU 25 million proposed in the common position of the Council represents only half of what the Commission proposed. That is what pushes the principle of budgetary austerity to the limits which correspond neither to the views of the Commission nor to those of Parliament in this matter. That is why, at the Council in June I fought to the end in favour of our proposal.

Without even talking about a real increase in the funds available the sums proposed by the Council would not even cover the rate of year‐on‐year inflation. The Commission, in the opinion which it has just sent to the Parliament and to the Council, has firmly rejected this amount. Parliament, by proposing an increase of ECU 100 million at the first reading, has shown how much it acknowledges the impact of Socrates both for the citizens and in the perspective of the long term development of the Union. The Commission, at the presentation of its amended proposal, took into account almost all of the amendments proposed by Parliament.

Mr President, ladies and gentlemen, it is true that the Commission"s proposal relating to the amount of increase is modest. We recognized this by declaring clearly in the explanation of our motives, and I quote: in a more favourable global budgetary context the Commission would not have hesitated to propose a much larger increase, which would have been fully justified by the ratio between costs and advantages of this activity and the necessity of establishing extra funding.

Nevertheless for 1998 the Commission has adopted a preliminary draft budget which proposes an increase limited to 2.4 % of committed credits by comparison with 1997. Bearing in mind the sums which will go to the structural activities to meet the commitments of the inter‐institutional agreement of October 1993, this severity imposes an extremely low increase under the other expenditure headings. For all the internal policies it is limited to 0.5 % by comparison with 1997. Such a limitation in the increase in the funding of internal policies did not enable us to propose as significant a revision of the Socrates programme as desired and in relation to the importance and success of this programme.

The Commission sees itself as constrained to continue to support a position of austerity. But you can be sure that your commitment is shared by the Commission. We shall fight to the last for a significant increase which will enable the programme to meet its goals in the coming years. First we shall redouble our efforts to convince the Council to soften its position between now and the Education Council on 20 November. Nevertheless it is most probable, as we all know, that we shall have to put in hand a procedure of conciliation.

I can assure you that the Commission is ready to play fully the role accorded to it by the treaty in the context of this procedure. We are going to use every means, in close collaboration with the Parliament, to obtain a result which is as favourable as possible.

Allow me therefore, in conclusion, to cast a glance at the future. It is in fact at this moment when we approach the planning work for the next generation of Community programmes put into operation from the year 2000. The Commission will at the beginning of November adopt a communication comprising the guidelines which we intend to follow in our work. This will give us another opportunity to exchange ideas on the future of this most important sector.

  President . – Thank you very much, Madam Commissioner.

The debate is closed.

The vote will take place tomorrow morning.(1)

(The sitting was closed at 11.17 p.m.)

(1) Agenda for next sitting: see Minutes

ANNEX

1. Questions to the Council

 

Question No 2 by María Izquierdo Rojo (H−0686/97)

Subject: Treatment of Moroccan citizens applying for visas

Is the Council aware that Moroccan citizens – professional people, teachers, doctors, distinguished writers, artists, etc. – are often subject to contemptuous and humiliating treatment when they apply for visas for temporary visits to the European Union?

What justification is there for so many errors and shortcomings? Is the Council aware that all this has prompted many protests?

Answer

The Council has no knowledge of the visa‐issuing practices referred to by the honourable Member, which might give rise to numerous protests and merit being described as dysfunction.

In fact nationals of the Kingdom of Morocco require visas when they cross the frontier of a Member State by virtue of Regulation 2317/95.

I would moreover like to take the opportunity to bring to the attention of the honourable Member that, as for any other arrangement decreed by the treaty, it is the Member States who are responsible for the application of the regulation and it is up to the Commission to be vigilant that it is applied correctly.

Question No 13 by Mihail Papayannakis (H−0720/97)

Subject: Turkish national land register and properties on the islands of Imbros and Tenedos

Using the new Turkish national land register as a pretext, the Turkish authorities do not recognize the acquisition, possession or conveyancing of title‐deeds to the property of the Greek population of Imbros and Tenedos unless they have Turkish title deeds, which do not exist because until 1960 they were not needed for transferring land. Furthermore, there is no recognition of properties which the Turkish authorities have classified as monastery property; accordingly, almost all Greek property appears on the land register as belonging to the Turkish State because, according to a decree issued by Sultan Suleyman, the entire island of Imbros is monastery property. Since the Council has stated (July 1995) that the EU will attempt to resolve the problems of ethnic groups, including those of Imbros, on the basis of the principles of the rule of law and the international commitments of the interested parties and, given that the new Turkish land register is not consistent with Article 14 of the Treaty of Lausanne concerning the protection of the indigenous Greek population and their property, will the Council say what representations it will make to the Turkish authorities to protect the property of the Greeks of Imbros and Tenedos, which appears in the new land register, under various pretexts, to belong to the Turkish State?

Answer

The Council regrets that property rights of a non‐Muslim minority guaranteed by an international treaty should be taken hostage by the poor climate of current relations between Turkey and a Member State. In its declaration of 15 July 1996 the Council considered that relations between Turkey and the EU had to be based on a clear commitment to respect international law and agreements.

In addition, at the meeting of the Joint Council with Turkey on 29 April 1997 the EU emphasized that the pursuit of stronger relations between the EU and Turkey depended on a number of factors, in particular the question of respect for human rights and the improvement of relations between certain Member States of the EU and Turkey by virtue of the settlement of their claims in accordance with international law, including by such means as recourse to the International Court of Justice.

Nevertheless, for the sake of clarity, it seems useful to emphasize that, in this particular case, it is more a question of a legal problem in international law than a problem related to the human rights situation.

Question No 14 by Richard Howitt (H−0721/97)

Subject: Human Rights in Colombia

Is the Council aware that in Colombia numerous self‐defence groups referred to as “convivir’ have been created which reportedly officially receive arms from the Colombian government.

The representative of the office of the UN High Commission for Human Rights recently requested the Colombian government that the law that permits the existence of the “convivir’ self‐defence groups be dismantled.

As this office receives funding from the European Union, does the Council support the recommendation of the UN High Commission? If this is the case, what steps has the Council taken to support the UN High Commission's recommendation?

Question No 15 by Luciana Castellina (H−0761/97)

Subject: Human rights in Colombia

Colombia is the country where the security forces, either directly, or through the intermediary of paramilitary groups, commit the worst human rights violations in all the countries of Latin America. European citizens have also be severely affected by these abuses, in particular an Italian national, Mr Turra, whose attackers were not prosecuted until Italy had threatened to review the trading privileges granted to Colombia by the Union.

How is the Council applying its 28 November 1991 resolution on human rights(1) , democracy and development, and in particular article 6 thereof, concerning serious and persistent human rights violations, to that country?

Question No 16 by Laura González Álvarez (H−0762/97)

Subject: Human rights in Colombia

On 24 October 1996(2) , Parliament adopted a political resolution on Colombia which referred, inter alia, to acts of violence perpetrated by paramilitary groups at the Bellacruz estate. Less than a fortnight later the Colombian Ambassador in Brussels and the joint owner of the estate, Mr Marulanda, had to resign from his post. Since then, many peasant families have had to move to the centre of the country and some key witnesses to coordinated operations involving both military and paramilitary forces have had to seek refuge in a European country.

Can the Council provide information regarding the investigations carried out and the possible legal penalties imposed on the people who murdered 37 peasants in less than a year in connection with this land conflict?

Question No 17 by Wolfgang Kreissl‐Dörfler (H−0767/97)

Subject: Trade union rights in Colombia

Many trade unionists have been convicted in Colombia by a procedure known as “faceless justice’, meaning that the accused is arraigned not before a judge but before a device that merely transmits the judge's distorted voice. This procedure is normally used for drug dealers, and its application to trade unionists is a flagrant violation of the ILO conventions on trade union rights.

Can the Council give the European Parliament any more detailed information on the free exercise of trade union rights in this country?

Joint Answer

Allow me, Mr President, to reply at the same time to the questions put by Mrs Castellina, Mrs Gonzales Alvarez, Mr Howitt and Mr Kreissl‐Dörfler, all four of which relate to the human rights situation in Colombia.

On this matter I must stress first of all that the Council recently made a further public statement, on 25 September, in the form of a declaration by the Presidency on behalf of the European Union, conveying its deep concern about the current human rights situation in Colombia. The Council is willing to respond positively to any appropriate request likely to facilitate the peace process in that country.

In that declaration the Union issued an urgent appeal to all the parties concerned to reach a negotiated settlement of this internal conflict, to respect both international humanitarian law and human rights and to avoid any violent action which might endanger the peace process.

On the same occasion the EU expressed its profound concern at the threats made by the guerillas and the paramilitary groups against candidates taking part in the elections and at the murders of certain candidates.

The question of the role and legality of the “convivir’ groups is currently being investigated by the Colombian Constitutional Court. It would therefore not be appropriate at this stage to make a declaration on this matter.

The Union also reiterated its support for the recommendations submitted to the United Nations Human Rights Commission in the reports drawn up by the special rapporteurs further to visits to Columbia.

In this context the Union explicitly gave its full support to the United Nations Human Rights Bureau and to the important mission with which it has been entrusted.

Furthermore, it was decided that the ambassadors of the Member States of the Union will continue to visit the regions of Colombia in which the most serious human rights problems have arisen in order to monitor the situation on the spot and to identify any areas in which the EU might provide its support.

The Council will continue to watch the human rights situation in Colombia very closely, particularly the problem of impunity and the freedom to engage in trade union activity.

Question No 18 by Bernd Posselt (H−0722/97)

Subject: Development in Latvia and Lithuania

In the light of the forthcoming Luxembourg summit how does the Luxembourg Presidency assess economic and political development in Latvia and Lithuania and does it see any possibility that, in view of the latest data which are not yet contained in the Commission report, these two countries might catch up with Estonia and enter the first round of potential new members?

Answer

In accordance with the conclusions of the European Council in Amsterdam the Commission submitted to the Council in July 1997 its opinion on the application for membership by countries of central and eastern Europe.

These opinions are currently being examined by the authorities of the Council. The decision on the countries with which negotiations will begin will be taken in December next by the European Council in Luxembourg, which will also deal with the whole process of enlargement. Work already done makes it pertinent to indicate that the opinions of the Commission are based on the most recent information available. Because of recent developments in the applicant countries it will also be opportune to follow closely the development of the situation so that account can be taken of any new relevant factor affecting considerations prior to the European Council in Luxembourg next December. It is clear that it must be a question of developments of a permanent nature and not reflect partial or cyclical evolutions.

It is also appropriate to reflect that enlargement is a process which is both inclusive and evolutional, none of the applicant countries being excluded. In this respect the European Council will be called upon to make a statement about the reinforcement of the pre‐accession strategy. In this context it is appropriate to note that the Commission proposes to present reports periodically on the subsequent progress made by each applicant country, reserving the right to make appropriate recommendations to the Council in the context of the enlargement process.

Question No 19 by Ursula Schleicher (H−0723/97)

Subject: Use of article 43 and article 100(a) as legal bases

As a consequence of the BSE crisis, defects in EU legislation also came to light. The Commission therefore gave an undertaking that in future all Community legal acts relating to foodstuffs and health would be based on article 100a of the EC Treaty. The Council did not follow suit (see the directive on the fixing of maximum levels for pesticide residues in and on fruit and vegetables etc.). The Council is at present seeking to base the proposed directive on veterinary checks on products entering the Community from third countries (COM 97/321) on article 43 of the EC Treaty despite Parliament and the Commission's demands to the contrary.

Does the Council believe that this will enhance the credibility of the EU 's efforts to limit the risks represented by BSE?

Answer

In reply to the honourable Member"s question, the Council recalls that, in accordance with Court of Justice precedent, the choice of the legal basis of an act may not depend solely on the view of an institution regarding the goal being pursued, but must be based on objective factors liable to be subjected to legal examination. These factors include, in particular, the aim and content of the act.

So in order to decide the appropriate legal basis of each proposal which is received by the Commission, the Council examines its aim and its content. Where the proposal concerns the production and marketing of agricultural products listed in the annex to the treaty and contributes to the achievement of one or more objectives of the common agricultural policy set out in article 39 of the treaty. The Council basis the act on article 43 in accordance with the law of the Court of Justice.

According to this law, even if the legislation in question covers at one and the same time the objectives of the common agricultural policy and other objectives, which in the absence of specific arrangements are pursued on the basis of article 100a of the treaty, no argument may be drawn from this article to limit the scope of article 43 of the treaty. In addition the pursuit of objectives of the common agricultural policy cannot disregard requirements of general interest, such as the protection of consumers, or the health and life of persons or animals.

With regard to the proposed directives of the Council fixing the principles relating to the organisation of veterinary checks for products originating in third countries and brought into the Community, the Council is of the opinion that in accordance with court precedent, article 43 constitutes an appropriate and sufficient legal basis.

A group has been set up to prepare the two agreements prescribed in article 6, namely:

‐ an agreement to be concluded by the Council with Norway and Iceland, determining the procedures by which these two countries will join in the operation and development of the Schengen “acquis’ and in the financial consequences of this development.

‐ an agreement to be concluded by the Council with Norway and Iceland establishing the rights and obligations between them and the United Kingdom and Ireland in the areas of the Schengen “acquis’ which apply to these latter states.

Questions like those raised by the honourable Member will probably be discussed in the context of this work.

***

Question No 20 by Pat Gallagher (H−0729/97)

Subject: Rockall fishery

Can the Council outline the practical effects for the EU's fishery fleet and in particular the Irish fleet, as a result of the UK Government's decision to ratify the United Nations Convention on the Law of the Sea, thereby ending its claim to control the waters off Rockall?

Answer

The instrument of accession to the United Nations Convention on the Law of the Sea deposited by the United Kingdom contains a commitment to accept the rights and obligations arising from that instrument.

As regards the question of the role to be attributed to rocks in the process of delimiting the continental shelf and the exclusive economic zones, article 121 (3) of the Convention provides that rocks which cannot sustain human habitation or economic life of their own shall have no exclusive economic zone or continental shelf. It follows therefore that the presence of a rock – in this case Rockall – has no influence over the delimitation of the aforementioned waters.

Subject: Norway, Iceland and the Schengen Agreement

Norway and Iceland have associate status as regards the Schengen Agreement. Once Schengen is incorporated into the Treaty on European Union, matters falling within the scope of the agreement will be dealt with by the EU Council of Ministers.

Will representatives of Norway and Iceland be able to attend Council meetings with a right to speak and submit proposals whenever Schengen‐related matters are discussed?

At its meeting of 16 October 1997, the Committee of Permanent Representatives defined the arrangements for the negotiations with Norway and Iceland on the basis of article 6 of the protocol integrating the Schengen “acquis’ into the framework of the European Union.

Question No 22 by Brian Crowley (H−0738/97)

Question No 23 by Maj Theorin (H−0740/97)

Subject: Illegal immigration

Illegal immigration is rising as Europe's asylum policies become increasingly restrictive. In 1990, 30, 000 people were refused entry to Europe. In 1993, this figure was 200, 000. Nevertheless, only one in five people are prevented from entering. Between 1991 and 1995, an estimated three million people came to Europe. It is illegal immigration that is now rising the fastest, according to Chris de Stoop, a Flemish journalist.

What action is the Council prepared to take in order to reduce the level of illegal immigration?

Answer

The Council is aware of the fact that clandestine immigration constitutes an important problem for the European Union and its Member States and must be combatted effectively in order to avoid undesirable consequences for our states, our societies but also for the individual immigrants who frequently leave home full of hope and at considerable personal sacrifice and who then find themselves deceived, victims of the traffic in human cargo which is increasingly taking the form of organized international crime.

In the past the Council has, in particular, adopted the following measures:

‐ recommendation on the harmonization of means of combatting illegal immigration and employment and the improvement of the means of control to that effect;

‐ recommendation relating to liaison and cooperation in carrying out removal measures;

‐ recommendation on countering illegal employment of nationals of third countries.

At the moment the Council is monitoring the operation of these measures. When the time comes it will consider the possibility of improving this operation or adding to these measures.

In addition the Council is currently working to improve the collection of information, its analysis and exchange between the Member States, These exchanges also involve certain third countries, notably the countries of central and eastern Europe. The objective pursued in this context is to be better able to deal with clandestine immigration by supplementing, where necessary, the aforementioned measures.

Illegal migration being, by definition, a cross‐border and international phenomenon, the European Union is strengthening cooperation with European third countries and certain overseas countries in the context of the Budapest process. It is precisely in this context that on 14 and 15 October 1997 a ministerial conference was held which was dedicated specifically to the prevention of illegal migration.

This subject also plays an important role in different areas, such as the structured dialogue with associated countries, the transatlantic dialogue and the Barcelona process with the Mediterranean countries.

The European Union and its Member States are nevertheless striving to identify means which will enable them to fight effectively against operators of the trade in human cargo.

The common policy of visas and in particular Regulation 2317/95 specifying the countries whose nationals are subject to the visa requirement, and the 1996 joint action relating to the airport transit system, constitute another method of controlling the flow of migrants.

Finally, the Council pays particular attention to the problem of readmission into the territory of the European Union, the origin of which may be found in the fact that a number of third countries refuse to take back their nationals, in violation of international law.

Thus the Council has adopted:

‐ a recommendation concerning a standard bilateral agreement for readmission between a Member State of the European Union and a third country;

‐ a recommendation concerning the guiding principles to be followed when drawing up protocols on the operation of readmission agreements,

‐ conclusions concerning the readmission clauses to be inserted in joint agreements to be made between a third country on the one hand and the European Community and the Member States on the other hand.

Nevertheless, in this sphere, the Council does not limit its activities to the drawing up of measures against illegal immigration. Quite the opposite, measures relating to the admission of persons, particularly the integration of persons residing legally in a Member State of the European Union, are regarded as very important by the Council.

***

Question No 24 by MaLou Lindholm (H−0743/97)

Subject: Equality in the EC Staff Regulations

On 2 October, an appeal was lodged with the court of first instance concerning discrimination against same‐sex couples in the Communities' Staff Regulations. On 30 September 1997, the Advocate‐General noted in his opinion in a case involving a similar problem (C−249/96) that discrimination against same‐sex partners (spouses) when granting allowances and other benefits to officials was incompatible with article 119 of the treaty. The legal and ethical basis for amending the Staff Regulations may already be considered to exist, but it was reinforced when, in signing the Treaty of Amsterdam on 2 October 1997, the Members of the Council supported the prohibition of discrimination, pursuant to the new article 6a. The idea of redefining the basis for granting allowances and other benefits, which should be 'partnership', is also supported by developments in several national legal systems. Is the Council prepared to take the political decision to amend the Staff Regulations in accordance with the European Parliament's position on this matter, during the current Presidency and without waiting for the Court of Justice to take its decision?

Answer

The Council can confirm the fact that legal actions are pending before the court of first instance and the Court of Justice, as mentioned in the honourable Member"s question.

A modification of status affecting the question posed by the honourable Member is currently being discussed in the context of the deliberations of Council on a proposed rule changing the status of civil servants of the European Communities and the regime applicable to other agents of these Communities in the matter of equality of treatment.

The original proposal was passed to the Council in 1993. Following initial consultation of the European Parliament the Commission, in a letter dated 6 March 1996 passed to the Council the text of a modified proposal (COM (96)77, final), on which the European Parliament gave a further opinion on 20 February 1997.

During the first half of 1997 an initial exchange of views on the modified proposal took place between the relevant Council authorities, during which it became necessary to defer the examination of the proposal until after the signature of the Treaty of Amsterdam.

The Luxembourg Presidency will supervise the carrying out of the examination by the Council of the Commission"s modified proposal in the light of the Treaty of Amsterdam and the amendments proposed by Parliament.

Since the Council is currently considering the matter it is not now in a position to make a statement, At this stage, on the course it wishes this dossier to take.

Question No 25 by David Martin (H−0747/97)

Subject: Recovery of unpaid duty from EU importers of Bangladeshi textiles

As a result of the cancellation of textile origin certificates by the Bangladeshi Government the Commission has instructed the customs authorities of the Member States to recover unpaid custom duties from European importers. Many of these importers could now face bankruptcy putting thousands of European jobs at risk.

When will the Council discuss this matter?

Question No 26 by Alex Smith (H−0758/97)

Subject: GSP Textile import certificates from Bangladesh

The invalidation of GSP import certificates from Bangladesh will have a severe impact on the mainly small‐scale EU textile importers, who are now faced with min. US$ 60 million in retroactive duties on imports between 1994 and 1996. That it is unfair to claim the duties in cases where EU importers have clearly acted in “good faith’ was recognized by the Council in May 1996 when it requested the Commission to find a solution for this. The Commission's reply, in the form of a Notice on the management of GSP, however, does not contain any proposal for improving the protection of importers acting in “good faith’.

Would the Council agree that it again needs to insist that the Commission puts forward proposals to deal with this problem? Would the Council also agree that in the meantime the post‐collection of duties from EU importers in the Bangladesh case should be waived?

Joint answer

During its consideration of the proposal submitted to the Council by the Commission in October 1995 with a view to amending Regulation (EC) N0 2913/92 establishing the Community Customs Code(3) , the Council held a lengthy debate on the problem faced by traders as a result of a situation in which they cannot reasonably claim any irregularity in the action taken by the authorities of non‐member countries and which, as the law stands at present, can only be rectified by charging the customs debt to those traders.

As a result of its discussions, the Council adopted, on 28 May 1996, together with its common position, a decision based on article 152 of the treaty on the post‐clearance recovery of customs debt (OJ C 170, 14.6.1996, p.1) and which the Council requested the Commission to carry out a detailed study with the view to finding an overall solution to the problems arising in the area of debt recovery and to submit an appropriate proposal to it on the matter by 31 December 1996. The fact that this decision was based on article 152 is in itself exceptional and demonstrates the Council"s interest in this matter and the importance which it attaches to the search for a fair, balanced, solution to these problems. A formal Commission proposal is obviously an indispensable condition for any amendment of the rules currently in force and the Council hopes that, as promised by the Commission"s departments, a new proposal amending the Community Customs Code will be submitted in the next few weeks.

Question No 28 by Vassilis Ephremidis (H−0763/97)

Question No 29 by Ioannis Theonas (H−0769/97)

Subject: Release of Souha Bechara and 200 Lebanese detainees from Khiam prison

Over 200 Lebanese resistance fighters are being held and tortured at Khiam, an interrogation and detention centre in southern Lebanon in the area occupied by Israel since 1978, and many of them have not been charged. Among the detainees is Souha Bechara who has been held in the same prison since November 1988 without being charged or put on trial. The conditions under which Souha Bechara and her fellow‐prisoners are being held are appalling and constitute a violation of all the international conventions on the dignity of the human person and human rights.

Bearing in mind that the European Parliament adopted a resolution in 1989 calling for the immediate release of Souha Bechara, will the Council say what initiatives it intends to take to ensure the immediate release of Souha Bechara and her fellow‐prisoners and the immediate implementation by Israel of UN Security Council Resolution 425 on the withdrawal of the occupying forces from Lebanon?

Answer

The Council shares the concern of the honourable Member about the humanitarian and legal position of detainees in South Lebanon.

The question posed refers to the particular case of Souha Fawaz Bechara. Unfortunately the Council has very little information on this matter at the moment. Ms Bechara is said to be the only woman still detained in Khiam where she has been held since November 1988. According to certain sources her family was authorized to see her in 1995 and, for some time, her mother had authorization to visit her once a month.

The Council wishes to emphasize the particular importance it attaches to the rights of detainees being respected and the relevant international conventions being applied. The protection and promotion of human rights constitutes an essential element in the relations between the Union and its Mediterranean partners, including Israel, and the Council will not spare its efforts in this sphere. Since it does not have at its disposal detailed information on the case of Souha Bechara, the Council would be grateful if the honourable Member would supply any additional useful detail in this matter.

As for the second part of the question, the Council has reaffirmed several times its support for the independence, the territorial integrity and the sovereignty of Lebanon within its internationally recognized borders, together with the integral operation of resolutions of the United Nations Security Council, in particular resolution 425.

I can assure the honourable Member that in respect of the Middle East peace process, in particular relating to Lebanon, the Council remains determined to pursue resolutely all its efforts to reach a just and global peace settlement for the whole region.

2. Questions to the Commission

 

Question No 47 by Ulf Holm (H−0754/97)

Subject: Sustainable development in the Treaty of Amsterdam

The new Treaty of Amsterdam has made sustainable development one of the EU's fundamental objectives. If its words are to give environmental matters prominence in the EU, the institutions responsible for initiating legislation must have a clear idea about what sustainable development entails and how it is to be promoted.

Will the Commission therefore explain:

How it defines sustainable development and what it has in mind when it uses the term?

Whether sustainable development can correctly be interpreted as meaning that 'the needs of present generations must not be met at the expense of future generations'?

How it intends to promote this objective in practice?

Answer

The Commission attaches great importance to sustainable development and has recognized the definition of this concept in the report of the World Commission on the Environment and Development, the Brundtland Report. The definition given, and universally accepted, is that of development able to meet the needs of present generations without compromising the possibility of meeting the needs of generations to come. This principle has been translated into the 5th Community action programme on the environment and sustainable development, adopted in 1992 in parallel with the Rio conference, which completes the definition in the following terms: a policy and a strategy designed to ensure continuity in time of economic and social development in respect of the environment and without compromising the natural resources indispensable to human activity.

Since the Commission has developed actions planned to translate into reality the concept of sustainable development. Thus, an evaluation in 1996 of the operation of the 5th Programme ended with a proposed plan of action at present before the Council and Parliament for adoption.

Amongst the examples of considering the environment in other policies, it is appropriate to cite the structural funds reform of 1993 and the 1996 communication on Cohesion Policy and the Environment; the Leader initiative in the sphere of rural development, the White Paper of 1995 on energy policy, the Green Paper of 1995: Towards fair and efficient tariff‐setting in transport, the communication on the Industrial Competitiveness of European Industry, and the consideration for the environment in development projects, especially in the context of the Phare programme and the programmes for developing countries.

This approach is going to be strengthened in the future. The Commission is delighted that the Treaty of Amsterdam is taking this direction by reinforcing the dimension of sustainable development in Community policies by a range of complementary arrangements. In particular, in accordance with article 6 of the new text, the principle of integrating the environment into other policies comes into the definition of sustainable development, and in any case constitutes one of the means of making it work. That is, of course, the Commission intention and the way it interprets Declaration No12 annexed to the treaty, which provides for Commission proposals liable to have a significant impact on the environment to be compulsorily assessed for such an impact.

At the internal level, the Commission has already taken steps by adopting, in July 1997, an internal communication on the integration of environmental considerations into the definition and management of policies. Amongst these measures the general directions must draw up environmental and sectorial policy statements and carry out assessments of the environmental impact of proposals which might have a significant impact, they must appoint personnel responsible for integration, and draw up an annual report on environmental performance. They will also develop ecological approaches to the operation of the Commission (“green housekeeping’) as well as the Community budget (“greening the budget’)

***

Question No 48 by Ioannis Theonas (H−0764/97)

Subject: Danger of pollution of the Mediterranean from nuclear and other toxic waste

Following the discovery of a large quantity of nuclear and other toxic waste produced in Europe on the sea bed, it is clear that the Mediterranean faces the danger of catastrophic pollution. According to Greenpeace whose allegations have been corroborated by the Italian judicial authorities, this unacceptable state of affairs is the work of multinationals which ship and store nuclear and other toxic waste in the Mediterranean and throughout the rest of the world. It is also alleged that these multinationals, and notably Oceanic Disposal Management, a subsidiary of an organisation called The Network, have links with the Mafia for the exploitation of this trade which has a turnover of many millions of dollars.

Will the Commission say what measures it intends to take to protect the Mediterranean from these Mafia activities which involve sinking ships full of toxic waste, to ensure strict compliance with Community directives and to ban the shipment and storage of nuclear and other toxic waste far from the place of production?

Answer

The Commission has no knowledge of any nuclear (radioactive) or toxic (hazardous) waste being stored in the Mediterranean.

The dumping of all ‚radioactive wastes or other radioactive matter’ was suspended under the Convention on the Prevention of Marine Pollution by Dumping of Wastes and Other Matter (London Convention 1972 – as amended in 1978, 1980 and 1993)(4) .

Toxic or hazardous waste may under Community law be disposed in the sea only with express authorization given by the competent authorities. The Barcelona Convention on the protection of the Mediterranean, to which the Community is a party, contains similar provisions, which in part are even more severe.

The transport of radioactive waste between Member States and into and out of the Community is covered by Council Directive 92/3/EURATOM. The transport of toxic (hazardous) waste is covered by Council Regulation (EEC) 259/93.

***

Question No 52 by Maj Theorin (H−0692/97)

Subject: Sale of alcopops

Alcopops are alcoholic beverages targeted mainly at children and young people. Colourful labels and 'cool' brand names entice young people to buy beverages in which the taste of alcohol is masked by the sweet soft‐drink flavour. We know that high alcohol consumption is particularly harmful to the young, and that future alcohol habits are formed in early years. Does the Commission therefore consider that a Member State is entitled on public health grounds to take action to remove alcopops from the free movement of goods in the internal market?

Answer

The Commissioner agrees with the honourable Member that alcopops represent a serious public health problem that needs to be tackled. There is nothing new about young people drinking, but the arrival of alcopops does change the situation.

Although drinking patterns differ across the Community, the drinks industry operates internationally. Fashionable activities spread rapidly among the young.

It can be predicted that alcopops and designer drinks will spread to all Member States and the UK experience suggests that market penetration can be very swift. Member States which have not yet had to face the issue, may find themselves dealing at very short notice with high‐strength drinks targeted at younger teenagers. It may be useful at European level to help:

‐ Raise awareness of the products and their potential impact on domestic markets and behaviour

‐ Share information on techniques to reduce the impact on children

‐ and to seek the agreement of manufacturers to abide by the same standards of packaging and marketing conduct in all Member States.

These actions very much reflect the position of the written declaration on alcopops and designer drinks which was brought forward by Mrs Eryl McNally MEP, and which has been signed by more than 200 Members of the European Parliament.

The Commission is looking at the existing experiences in the Member States, for example the new code of practice concluded in the UK, with a view to establishing whether lessons can be drawn from them.

The Commission is looking very seriously at the underlying issue raised by the honourable Member.

***

Question No 53 by Hugh McMahon (H−0703/97)

Subject: Future development of social dialogue and operation of the social protocol

Will the Commission inform Parliament how it intends to develop the social dialogue following on from the incorporation of the social protocol into the main body of the Treaty following the UK Government's decision to end the social policy opt‐out during the Amsterdam negotiations?

In particular, what are to be the procedures for legislation moving from the negotiation stage to the legislative stage and what role does the Commission envisage for Parliament in this process?

Answer

At the Amsterdam European Council the provisions of the Agreement on social policy currently attached to the treaty through the Social Protocol were incorporated into the new Amsterdam Treaty. This means that all 15 Member States now can proceed together to strengthen European social policy.

This also means a major strengthening of the social dialogue, as the new treaty states that the commission has the task of promoting the consultation of the Social Partners at Community level and taking any relevant measures to facilitate their dialogue. Specifically, the Social Partners shall be consulted on the possible direction of Community action before any proposals are made in the social policy field. The Commission has been doing this informally for some time, but the new treaty is a formal recognition of the role of management and labour in this domain.

In order to fulfil its task, the Commission will present the Council and Parliament with a communication on the future of the social dialogue before the end of this year. The Commission will in this document propose a number of concrete measures on how to strengthen the social dialogue and to associate it more closely in the development and implementation of Community policies.

The communication will be the result of a comprehensive consultation exercise with the European Institutions, the Member States and the Social Partners. The European Parliament has contributed greatly to that exercise by its in‐depth analysis of the future of the social dialogue in its parliamentary resolution of 18 July this year.

In spite of pressure from the Commission and the European Parliament, however, no changes were made at the Amsterdam European Council as regards the involvement of the European Parliament in the consultation and negotiating process.

Honourable Members will be aware that up until now the Commission has kept the European Parliament fully informed and enabled it to express its views on the agreements concluded by the social dialogue under the Social Protocol. The Commission intends to maintain this practice for the time being and to see what more can be done to enable the European Parliament to play a constructive role in this process. In this context, it goes without saying that the terms of the Treaty and the autonomy of the Social Partners need to be respected.

***

Question No 54 by Edgar Schiedermeier (H−0731/97)

Subject: Social dialogue on part‐time work

Does the Commission consider the results of the social dialogue on part‐time work to be adequate and does it as a result expect progress in the employment of male and female workers?

Answer

The Commission considers part‐time work to be an important factor in promoting employment and equal opportunities for women and men and therefore welcomes the framework agreement concluded between UNICE CEEP and ETUC. The Commission has demonstrated its belief in its usefulness by making the proposal for a directive.

Part‐time work can contribute to the modernization of work organization and working patterns and thus plays a significant role in allowing enterprises to adapt to new technologies and new market conditions. The Commission, in its Guidelines for Member States Employment Policies, has identified this adaptability of enterprises as decisive for their viability and their ability to seize new business and new employment opportunities.

Any mechanism governing part‐time work must also take account of the interests of workers by enabling them to tailor their working hours more closely to their needs. The agreement on this matter, which results from negotiations between employers and workers, is the right vehicle for reconciling the interest of the two parties.

However, part‐time work is only one aspect of the broader issue concerning the protection and promotion of other flexible forms of work. In fact, the Commission consulted the social partners in the broader context of flexibility in employment and security for employees.

In the preamble of the framework agreement, the social partners have already expressed their intention to consider the need for similar agreements on other flexible forms of work. The Commission hopes that the social partners will soon be able to agree to open the relevant negotiations.

The Commission believes that by ensuring equality of treatment for part‐time and fixed‐term employees, it can make a real contribution to increasing employment, and the quality of that employment, for men and women.

Question No 55 by Richard Howitt (H−0716/97)

Subject: DG V Unit for external relations and codes of conduct

How far has the Commission DG V Unit for External Relations and International Organizations monitored the ILO Tripartite Declaration of Principles concerning Multinational Enterprises and Social Policy and the OECD Guidelines for Multinational Enterprises inside and outside Europe?

As it is the policy of the European Union that social justice and respect for basic human rights should go hand in hand with economic development, has the European Commission made any progress in developing a code of conduct for European corporations operating in developing countries?

Answer

This question raises the issue of international standards for the conduct of multinational enterprises. As this is an increasingly debated issue in various fora, the Commission follows it very closely.

The ILO Tripartite Declaration of Principles concerning Multinational Enterprises and Social Policy represents a consensual statement of principles and is a non‐binding instrument. There are regular reviews on its application which reflect the voluntary character of this declaration.

The Commission has also followed and participated in the discussions which have taken place within the OECD on the guidelines for multinational enterprises and particularly the case submitted for consideration to the Committee on International Investment and Multinational Enterprises (CIME).

At European level, the Commission has welcomed the Code of Conduct recently signed by the social partners in the European textile and clothing sector. This charter, which was negotiated under the auspices of the Commission, calls on their members to respect International Labour Organization conventions banning child labour, forced labour and discrimination and recognizing freedom of association and the right to negotiate. The charter also provides for a yearly evaluation of its implementation.

The Commission is open to study any further initiative aiming at promoting the respect of labour standards. In this context, it is clear that enterprises are becoming increasingly concerned about the social aspect of their activities and consumers are becoming better informed. The Commission will support and encourage European firms or sectors to adopt codes of conduct on a voluntary basis but in all such cases the Commission will be asking that a monitoring system be an integral part of the package.

Question No 56 by Alexandros Alavanos (H−0726/97)

Subject: Transfer of ESF appropriations to other funds

It has been publicly reported that the Greek Government is requesting the transfer from the ESF to other structural funds of about ECU 425 m which it does not think it will be able to utilize.

To what extent have ESF appropriations earmarked for Greece been utilized to date and what is the average rate of utilization in the other Member States? What does the Commission consider to be the reasons for the low rate of utilization of the ESF appropriations granted and which programmes are encountering the greatest problems? What view does the Commission take of the above request by the Greek Government?

Answer

The utilization of ESF appropriations earmarked for Greece in the 1994‐1999 CSF, amounted on 30 September 1997 to 42 % in terms of commitments and 31 % in terms of payments, compared to an average rate of 67 % and 43 % respectively for the Community as a whole.

The specific reasons for the low rate of utilization of the ESF include insufficient planning and implementation structures in the Member State; lack of relevant experience and necessary flexibility in management; and frequent changes of personnel responsible for programme implementation, especially in the early stages.

The problems vary from one programme to another. The human resources programmes with the lowest utilization rate of ESF appropriations are ‚Combating Exclusion from the Labour Market’, ’Modernization of Public Administration’ and ‚Education and Initial Training’ respectively.

However, implementation has accelerated and funds absorption has improved in 1997. In the case of the Education Programme, for example, overall absorption at the end of 1997 is expected to reach around 30 %.

A meeting of the Advisory Committee for Human Resources was held on 10 October 1997 in Athens. During this meeting new proposals were considered in the framework of the mid‐term review of the human resources operations.

Assurances were given by the Greek authorities of their determination to improve and accelerate implementation, and a number of important steps taken should prove helpful in particular the creation of a national structure for certification.

At the end of this meeting, a general consensus was reached that at this stage there is no need to modify the financial allocations of the human resources programmes.

It has also been agreed that the monitoring arrangements relating to ESF‐financed actions in Greece will be strengthened in order to promote rapid and effective execution of ESF funding.

Question No 57 by Georgios Anastassopoulos (H−0745/97)

Subject: Second CSF for Greece/Centres for European Languages

As part of the Second Community Support Framework for Greece, the Greek Ministry of Education has drawn up an action entitled 'Foreign Languages'. This provides for the foundation, organisation and operation of Centres for European Languages devoted to teaching.

Will the Commission say why has it acquiesced in the exclusion of the private foreign language teaching sector from Community co‐funding under the Second CSF?

Why has it consented to the creation of rigid and expensive administrative mechanisms which take a very long time to set up and operate and are of questionable effectiveness?

Why has it agreed to the attempt to marginalize a vigorous professional sector which has a particularly important contribution to make in teaching foreign languages and discriminated in favour of what is generally acknowledged to be a very weak foreign language teaching system which does not even know how to tap the potential of the public eduction sector with its thousands of unappointed teachers?

Answer

The question raised by the honourable Member refers to a particular action ‚Learning of Foreign Languages’ financed by the ESF through the Operational Programme ‚Initial Training and Education’, which forms part of a national policy for the provision of foreign languages education in Greece.

In view of the complex nature of the implementation arrangements, the Commission is in consultation with the Ministry of Education in order to clarify and further elaborate all aspects of this action.

Question No 58 by Brian Crowley (H−0749/97)

Subject: Working conditions of flight crew

Considering the fact that working conditions have an effect on aviation safety, and given that at the present time there is not any national legislation in Ireland governing the work time of flight crew, could the Commission report on whether it intends to introduce legislation regarding the harmonization of working conditions for flight crew and, if so, whether this will be legislated for under the provisions in the White Paper on the sectors excluded from the Working Time Directive or whether the Commission intends to introduce specific legislation to deal with the harmonization of working time for flight crew and, if so, when?

Answer

In the White Paper (COM(97) 334) on Sectors and Activities excluded from the Working Time Directive (93/104/EC), the Commission has made it clear that a pragmatic approach should be taken in order to ensure at Community level the protection of the health and safety, with regard to working time, of the workers concerned. Subject to views on the White Paper, a three‐pronged approach is proposed, which would have the following consequences for workers in the air transport sector.

First, the full provisions of the Working Time Directive would be extended to all non‐mobile workers, that is about 80 % of workers working for air transport companies.

Second, all flight and cabin crews would be covered by the provisions of the Working Time Directive on 4 weeks' paid annual leave and health assessments for night workers; provision would also be made to ensure that crews have adequate rest and for a maximum number of hours to be worked annually.

Third, the Commission is currently working on a proposal for a Flight Time Limitation Scheme, which will regulate operational safety requirements.

Following consideration of the comments on the White Paper, a document will be prepared with a view to a second stage consultation with the social partners early in 1998.

Question No 59 by Elly Plooij‐van Gorsel (H−0755/97)

Subject: Double premiums levied by France on the supplementary pensions of EU citizens

The answer to my Written Question E−2701/97 indicates that the Commission does not intend to abolish the double social premiums levied on supplementary pensions, which affect Dutch pensioners living in France, by amending Regulation 1408/71(5) .

Can the Commission set out the reasons for its refusal to propose new provisions on this matter or amendments to Regulation 1408/71, also in view of the Commission's position in the Court's ruling in Case C−57/90 and Articles 48 to 51 of the EC Treaty, which are intended to achieve the greatest possible freedom of movement for workers?

Can the Commission discover how many EU citizens who have settled in another Member State have to cope with double premiums levied on their supplementary pensions?

Answer

The Commission in sensitive to the honourable Member's concerns about the double levy of social contributions on pension benefits. However, as it has underlined in its answer to Written Question 2701/97 from the honourable Member, according to the case law of the European Court of Justice, occupational supplementary pension schemes do not fall within the scope of the Community regulations on social security for migrant workers. This means that the coordinating mechanism in this regulation cannot be used to resolve this particular problem.

Nonetheless, the double levy of social contributions by both the Dutch and the French authorities, and in particular the ‚contribution sociale generalisée’ might be considered to be a breach of article 8A or articles 48 to 51 of the EC Treaty and an obstacle to the free movement of workers. The Commission will include this element in its ongoing infringement proceedings against France concerning the CSG.

Moreover, the place of supplementary pension schemes in Regulation 1408/71 will be re‐examined in the framework of the current review and simplification of the regulation.

The Commission will also examine the number of persons involved in the problem raised by the honourable Member of Parliament.

Question No 60 by Liam Hyland (H−0760/97)

Subject: New technologies and access to training for older workers

In April 1997 a high‐level expert group presented its final report to the Commission on 'Building the European Information society for us all'.

What plans does the Commission now consider should be put in place at EU and national level aimed at ensuring that order workers are provided with equal opportunities vis‐a‐vis access to additional training and learning to keep up with the pace of changes in the information society and the new technologies?

Answer

The Commission shares with the High Level Expert Group the concern about the access of older workers to training in information and communication technologies. The European Labour force is ageing. About 20 % of the European workforce are over 50 and this percentage of older workers in the population will increase over the next few years. The probability of workers receiving training falls as the age of the workers increases.

In its Guidelines for Member States Employment Policies 1998 the Commission has emphasised the need to develop a culture of employability based in particular on lifelong learning. People have to be encouraged and helped to acquire new skills and competencies. Firms have also a contribution to make. Member States should adopt employability as a key objective of their employment policies.

The European Social Fund is contributing to maintaining the employability of older workers. Objective 4 and the Community Initiative Adapt are specifically aimed at facilitating the adaptation of workers to industrial change. The Leonardo da Vinci Programme also contributes by supporting innovative projects in this area.

France and Finland have specifically included older workers as a target group within their objective 4 actions. For the rest of the present programming period, the acquisition of information and communication technologies skills will be a priority in the context of both objectives 3 and 4.

For the next programming period, the Commission has proposed in the Agenda 2000 the creation of a new objective 3 especially devoted to human resources development and that will combine elements from current objectives 3 and 4. Within the action lines considered, the Commission will look in particular to maintain the employability of workers, including measures in favour of older workers, in the context of the information society.

Question No 61 by Thomas Mann (H−0684/97)

Subject: Commission representation in Katmandu

For some months now the European Union's South Asian partners have been promised that an office of the Commission will be opened in Katmandu. This office is intended to be a sign of solidarity with the seven member states of the South Asian Association for Regional Cooperation (SAARC).

The establishment of this office in the capital of Nepal, which is the seat of the SAARC Secretariat, is important in the interests of pursuing successful political, economic and cultural cooperation.

When can we expect this EU representation to be opened?

Answer

The Commission is already represented in 128 countries and several international organizations.

It is, however, aware that its network of external representation needs to be kept under constant review and, where necessary, extended to take account of the evolution of the Commission's relations with third countries.

In the case of Nepal, where the Commission's aid programme for the years 1991 to 1996 included, has amounted to about ECU 60 million, there is a case to be made for the opening of an Office dependent on the Delegation in New Delhi. Indeed there is already in Katmandu a technical office with 4 local agents recruited by the Delegation in New Delhi.

However, the situation of financial and human resources concerning the External Service are such that, at present, it is only possible to open new representations of the Commission by making the required extra resources available, by redeployment or by budgetary reinforcement.

Thus while the Commission has the matter under review it can give no undertaking on the opening of a representation in Katmandu or elsewhere.

Question No 62 by Manuel Medina Ortega (H−0685/97)

Subject: Compensation for seamen affected by biological rest periods under the agreement with Morocco

The owners of cephalopod fishing boats established in the Canary Islands have received substantial Community aid amounting to ECU 3.24 million to compensate them for the losses resulting from the extension of the biological rest period from two to four months. Has provision also been made for compensating the seamen affected by this rest period?

Answer

Spain has notified the Commission, on the basis of article 93 of the EC Treaty, of a ministerial decree which has been approved by decision of the Commission on 20 June 1997. This decree provides aid both for owners and crews of the cephalopod fishing fleet affected by the increase from two to four months of the biological rest period stipulated in the agreement with Morocco. The national measures may be cofinancial in the IFOP framework within the limits of article 14 of rule 3699/93 of the Council relating to the temporary halting of the fleet, since this is the first time that an extension of the rest periods to four months has been decided.

However, the state aid can no longer be permitted in the future for such temporary rests, since these could be considered as foreseeable and non‐exceptional.

Question No 63 by María Izquierdo Rojo (H−0687/97)

Subject: Overhead electric cables in the historic district of Albayzin in Granada

Is the Commission aware that there is still an overhead medium‐voltage electricity cable running between poles in the historic district of Albayzin in Granada (which has been declared a UNESCO world heritage site), and that this is an eyesore, ruining the environment and the landscape? Is this not clearly counterproductive in terms of public safety, aesthetic considerations, tourism, the environment, etc.?

Answer

In reply to the honourable Member"s question, the Commission recalls that its action in the sphere of safeguarding the cultural heritage based on article 128 of the EC Treaty is limited to supporting and complementing the actions of Member States. By virtue of the principle of subsidiarity it thus has no capacity to intervene in questions which refer exclusively to the competence of national or regional authorities of Member States, such as the presence of aerial electrical cables in an historic district.

The Commission nevertheless points out that it has informed the appropriate Spanish authorities of the question posed by the honourable Member.
