Question No 64 by Arthur Newens (H−0688/97)

Subject: The EU banana regime

What steps is the Commission planning to take to avert the most serious repercussions on small producers of bananas in the Caribbean and other areas in the light of the decision of the World Trade Organization's Disputes Panel on the EU Banana Regime.

Answer

The EC supports the WTO and its multilateral dispute settlement system, and it is the Commission's intention to meet the obligations under the WTO with respect to this matter. The Commission is carefully assessing the report of the Appellate Body, which appears to contain a number of contradictions and unclear statements. It is premature at this stage to draw conclusions on what course of action the Commission will follow in order to respect the international obligations.

To respect Community's commitments to the ACP is a fundamental objective. Indeed, even the Appellate Body has accepted that many of the actions towards the ACP stem from commitments under the Lomé Convention.

In accordance with normal procedures, the Parliament will of course be kept fully informed of the results of the Commission's analysis and any proposals for action which may result from it.

Question No 65 by Michael Tappin (H−0690/97)

Subject: Free movement of citizens

The principle of the free movement of European citizens is a cornerstone of the European Union. Yet the right of free, unimpeded movement is not available to all. There are examples of harassment impeding free movement – based on race, economics and, as the tragic accident which took place in Paris on 30 August involving three European citizens, Diana Princess of Wales, Mr Henri Paul and Mr Trevor Rees‐Jones showed, also celebrity status.

Would the Commission clarify what affirmative actions Member States are obliged to take to guarantee the right of free movement to all its citizens?

Answer

The Commission shares the honourable Member"s conviction: freedom of movement and residence for Union citizens is now a general and fundamental principle, pursuant to article 8a of the EC Treaty.

However, the Commission takes the view that the unfortunate and tragic accident to which the honourable Member refers does not raise issues which relate to freedom of movement and the exercise of that freedom within the meaning of Community law.

Question No 66 by Robin Teverson (H−0691/97)

Subject: Threat to the marine environment of Antarctica

Recent reports indicate a new and grave threat to the natural marine environment in Antarctica. Many fishing fleets are converging on the area to fish newly exploited and valuable species without paying regard to international treaty arrangements, for example, those of the Commission for the Conservation of the Antarctic Marine Living Resources (CCAMLR), of which the EU is a signatory.

What urgent initiatives will the Commission be taking in a concerted manner with other nation states to protect this fragile environment before it is too late?

Answer

The recent reported surge in the level of fishing activities in the CCAMLR Convention Area and in the high seas adjacent to this area, is a matter of great concern to the Commission and to CCAMLR's contracting parties, as such activities undermine the effectiveness of CCAMLR's conservation measures.

Evidence of such activities has resulted from reported sightings of fishing vessels in international waters and reported illegal activities, notably the EEZs of certain CCAMLR contracting parties.

This new activity in the Convention Area takes two forms. The first concerns vessels of CCAMLR's contracting parties which are not notified to CCAMLR as intending to fish in the Convention Area, and the second concerns vessels of non‐contracting parties which fish without restriction and disregard CCAMLR's conservation measures.

Regarding measures for addressing non‐notified fishing by vessels flying the flag of contracting parties, the Commission intends, in close cooperation with other interested parties, to urge the establishment of a Vessel Monitoring System (VMS), an initiative which the EC has strongly supported in the past.

In addition, the reinforcement of CCAMLR's System of Inspection may be considered through provision ensuring the minimal presence of inspection vessels in the fishing fleet, the definition of a ‚serious’ infringement, the rerouting of vessels upon consent of the Flag State, and the communication of the movements and the catches held on board (Hail System).

Regarding the fishing activities of non‐contracting parties in the Convention Area, the Commission will examine with other parties the possible adoption of measures of a commercial nature, such as the prohibition of transhipment and landings in the ports of contracting parties targeting vessels undermining CCAMLR's measures and the obligatory notification of landings and mandatory inspections in ports.

The Commission is in favour of increasing CCAMLR's mandate in issues pertaining to illegal fishing. In this framework, the Community should call for a stronger role for CCAMLR in that it may establish measures which will guarantee responsible fishing activities both within the Convention Area and in the high seas adjacent to it.

Question No 67 by Bryan Cassidy (H−0695/97)

Subject: Announcement in OJ for expressions of interest for the post of Member of one of the Scientific Committees

In the OJ publication C 233 dated 1 August 1997 the Commission announced that it is to restructure the six Scientific Committees in the fields of food, animal nutrition, pesticides, toxicity and ecotoxicity, veterinary public health and cosmetology, by setting up eight new Scientific Committees to replace these existing ones.

However, the deadline for the application was set for 19 September 1997. In view of the summer recess over the month of August, does the Commission consider that this deadline provides sufficient time for somebody to make an application?

Answer

The Commission is aware of the problem raised by the honourable Member.

However, in view of the undertaking given to Parliament as regards the reorganization of the Scientific Committees, on the basis of the principles set out in the Communication of 30 April entitled ‚Consumer health and food safety"(6) , the various stages in the procedure for the appointment of new members of the Scientific Committees have had to be carried out with some degree of urgency.

On 23 July 1997 the Commission adopted the decision establishing the eight Scientific Committees.(7) And, from the date onwards, the Commission had disseminated as widely as possible the invitation to experts in the consumer health field to express their interest in working on the Scientific Committees.

The closing date for the call for expressions of interest was set for 19 September 1997 precisely so that people on holiday in August would hear about it and could draw up their applications in good time.

The fact that 824 persons from all Member States and several third countries replied in good time to the call for expressions of interest published on 2 August 1997 suggests that that objective was achieved.

Question No 68 by Jonas Sjöstedt (H−0699/97)

Subject: The environment and the transit negotiations between the EU and Switzerland

The EU is currently negotiating a bilateral agreement with Switzerland on the conditions governing road haulage transit traffic through Switzerland. It has proved difficult to reach solution acceptable to both sides. This has affected EU‐Swiss cooperation in other fields. Has the Commission conducted any environmental analysis of the EU's demands on Switzerland? Which scheme for transit traffic, the Swiss or the EU proposal, does the Commission regard as the better one for the European environment?

Answer

One of the main objectives of the Community has been – and is – the creation of a stable, efficient and sustainable policy regime for transport across the entire Alpine region. In preparing the Community's negotiating directives with Switzerland, therefore, the Council paid particular attention to the need to establish a balance between environmental and economic objectives.

A full environmental analysis of the impact of any agreement will only be possible once the parameters of the negotiated solution have been determined. The Commission considers, however, that the approach taken by the Community strongly reflects environmental as well as economic considerations. An important instance of that is the effort to end the existing 28‐tonne truck limit in Switzerland.

That limit causes unacceptable traffic diversion through Austria and France and effectively halves the maximum load per lorry, thereby greatly increasing the number of journeys needed to carry the same tonnage.

While the Commission therefore wants to see the 28‐tonne limit abolished, it is agreed with the Swiss that this should occur in parallel with the introduction of a new charging system for road haulage at levels which are both economically and environmentally justifiable and do not result in a continuation of the same scale of diversions. As well as providing for road use, however, the agreement must also ensure that a satisfactory rail‐based alternative exists.

Question No 69 by Alfred Lomas (H−0710/97)

Subject: Effects of Agent Orange in Vietnam

In view of the continuing disastrous effects of the use of Agent Orange by U.S. troops in Vietnam – on the environment and on the population, including the children suffering horrendous birth defects, will the Commission consider increasing aid to Vietnam in order to give specific help to the victims of Agent Orange?

Answer

The Commission is aware of the disastrous effects which the use of Agent Orange during the Vietnam War had on the environment and the local population.

Bilateral talks are under way between the Vietnamese Government and the US Administration with a view to drawing up a relevant programme of practical measures.

The Commission has not yet been approached by the Vietnamese authorities, which seem to regard this problem primarily as a bilateral one which should be dealt with as a matter of priority in connection with the technical and financial assistance which the United States might grant Vietnam in the future.

However, the Commission is aware of the serious public health problems currently facing Vietnam and will provide aid totalling ECU 27 million for the development of the health system in that country.

Question No 70 by Christine Oddy (H−0712/97)

Subject: Hams Hall North Warwickshire, Rover and BMW

Is the Commission aware that a new BMW plant is to be built at Hams Hall North Warwickshire, UK and that this new initiative will safeguard 1500 existing jobs and has been partly facilitated by ERDF infrastructure support?

What steps will the Commission take to foster this important development, encourage creating world class engineering skills in the West Midlands area UK and improve and support West Midlands engineering and components industry to further greater links between BMW and the West Midlands?

Answer

The development at Hams Hall, North Warwickshire received assistance from the European Regional Development Fund (ERDF) under the West Midlands Objective 2 programme for 1994‐96. The project concerned the reclamation and servicing of a derelict, contaminated site and will enable the attraction of several major companies to the area, including Rover BMW.

Steps have in fact already been taken to foster this important development. Under that programme the Commission supported a major initiative to encourage the creation of a world class supplier base in the West Midlands with a total contribution from the Structural Funds of £10.5 million. The project focuses on improving the international competitiveness of the West Midlands automotive components supply chain. Over 4.000 supply companies will be involved in awareness raising, benchmarking and performance measurement with the long‐term goal of creating a culture of continuous improvement. The partnership undertaking this project brings together local authorities, universities and TECs and includes Rover and Jaguar. More than 1, 200 people will be trained under the scheme and some 27, 000 jobs sustained.

It is not for the Commission, through its European Regional Policy Programmes, to further the links between a region and individual firms. Support from the Structural Funds creates the right environment for industrial investment generally. The Commission will continue to support such developments through the new Objective programme for the West Midlands covering the period 1997‐99, which as a strong focus on support for SME development.

An application for aid has been made by Rover BMW under the national scheme of Selective Financial Assistance involving no contribution of ERDR. The aid application for the Rover BMW engine plant is currently under scrutiny by the Commission to ensure that the proposed aid complies with the Community rules on state aid.

Question No 71 by Nikitas Kaklamanis (H−0714/97)

Subject: Nuclear waste on the Turkish coast

According to allegations made by the Mayor of Bergama, Sefa Taskin, large amounts of toxic and nuclear waste are buried in the shafts of abandoned mines in the region of Balia in northwestern Anatolia and have already caused serious problems for local flora and fauna.

Together with 300 local inhabitants, the Turkish mayor attempted to approach these mines, but was the victim of an unprecedented attack by the gendarmerie which has hundreds of heavily‐armed men guarding the site.

Some months ago the Mayor and the inhabitants of Bergama closed down the gold ore processing plant which was seeking to operate in the region, since it was suspected of being a screen for the burial of toxic and nuclear waste.

In statement to the Turkish press, the Turkish mayor expressed doubts concerning the reasons given by the gendarmerie for refusing access to the area and stated that the whole matter had been rigged.

Will the Commission give its views on this matter and say how it intends to react, since the entire approach adopted by Turkey threatens the Aegean region and the islands with wholesale disaster, and amounts to contempt for the stringent environmental principles governing the EU which is providing Turkey with generous financial aid?

Answer

The Commission is aware of the problems associated with the environment in Turkey.

It is in this context that the resolution of the joint EC – Turkey Council of 6 March 1995 anticipated entering into cooperation in this sphere with Turkey with a view to reinforcing the fight against deterioration of the environment.

In the context of financial cooperation between the Commission and Turkey the Turkish authorities have also submitted to the Commission a plan relating to the evaluation and control of pollution of the sea, the soil and the air along the whole Mediterranean coastline of Turkey. The current situation of financial cooperation with Turkey does not permit an indication of whether such a project could be financed in the near future.

The Commission"s communication of 15 July 1997 on the future of relations with Turkey also proposes to place the emphasis on cooperation in the sphere of the environment, so as to engage a long term development in that country and to integrate this sector in the other economic policies: this kind of cooperation necessitates the participation of the Community budget.

In the immediate future, given that the facts referred to by the honourable Member concerning installations located outside Community territory, the Community directives do not apply.

Question No 72 by Philippe Monfils (H−0715/97)

Subject: Ban on the Bouglione Circus from performing in Ypres contrary to the principles of free competition and freedom of movement

The town of Ypres has banned the Bouglione Circus from performing there on the basis of a letter from the Flemish Government to all the Flemish local councils which states that only five circuses are recognized and supported by the Flemish community since they are established on Flemish territory and that growing competition from other, non‐recognized circuses might, therefore, adversely affect their position.

Are the Flemish Government and the town of Ypres not blatantly in breach of the principles of free competition and freedom of movement? Will the Commission intervene to put an end to this unacceptable discrimination?

Answer

The Commission thanks the honourable Member for putting this question, because, in fact, the situation referred to had not been brought to its notice.

In accordance with the principle of free provision of services contained in article 58 of the EC Treaty any economic operator must be able to carry on his activity in a Member State other than the one in which his operation is based without suffering discrimination associated with his nationality, nor must he encounter any hindrances.

Only measures based on reasons of public order, public safety or public health or on urgent reasons relating to the general interest and commensurate with the objective to be achieved may be justified.

The Commission will therefore take the necessary steps to verify the situation described and its compatibility with the Community"s principles. Should the need arise it will take the necessary measures to bring it to an end.

Question No 73 by Clive Needle (H−0725/97)

Subject: Citizens First Initiative

ECU 4 million was allocated from the public health budget line to support the Citizens First Initiative in 1996. Citizens First was launched one year ago. Public health and health and safety at work were scheduled to come on stream in April 1997 but were postponed to this autumn. There is still no visible sign of their introduction.

What has been done since the ECU 4 million was allocated? Can the Commission announce an initiative now that we have reached autumn 1997? If the appropriation is not spent on Citizens First, what will happen to it?

Answer

The first phase of the Citizens First Initiative, launched last year, covered the themes of working, living and studying in another Member State. It was funded entirely from the Prince budget line (Information Programme for the European Citizen).

The second phase, to be launched in November, will cover the themes of buying goods and services in the single market, travelling in the Community, and equal opportunities for women and men. this is also funded from the Prince line; in addition, and following a recommendation of the European Parliament, the funds for the 1997 Consumer Information Campaign are being spent in full association with the second phase.

Building on the experience of the last two years, the Single Market Action Plan, adopted at the Amsterdam European Council, provides for the establishment of a permanent Dialogue with the Citizen in 1998. Among the themes which are being considered for inclusion are aspects of public health, and health and safety at the workplace. At this stage it is not envisaged that funding for the dialogue would be drawn from the Public Health line, unless additional provision is included.

Question No 74 by Astrid Thors (H−0728/97)

Subject: Milk products in schools

At the September meeting of the Agriculture Council, it was decided, in response to a Commission proposal, to add soured milk (fil) to the list of products eligible for the school milk subsidy, as previously agreed. To the great dismay of the Finns, however, the Commission had also stipulated that the soured milk should be made from whole milk. This type of soured milk is not to Finnish tastes and accounts for only 8 % of the market, which is good, since it makes sense to reduce the fat content of dairy products and promote healthier eating habits.

According to reports, a complete revision of the school milk subsidy programme is planned, inter alia in order to take greater account of health aspects. How and when does the Commission intend to revise the programme in order to achieve that aim and give Member States more scope to decide on the fine detail of the programme?

Answer

As indicated by the honourable Member, the Commission's proposal to include certain types of fermented milk in the list of dairy products eligible for aid under the school milk scheme, resulted from a request by the Council as part of its decision on the 1997/98 price package. The Commission's proposal was in line with this decision and, moreover, it is consistent with the policy on the school milk scheme in general.

A thorough revision of the Community school milk programme was undertaken in 1993. One of the main aims of this revision was to increase the efficiency of the scheme by reducing the number of eligible dairy products and by giving more flexibility to Member States for implementing the rules, taking account of their specific situation. The Commission does not currently intend to make a proposal to review the scheme once again. It will, however, continue to evaluate the situation in the light of the treaty requirement that a high level of human health protection shall form a constituent part of the Community's other policies.

Question No 75 by Patricia McKenna (H−0733/97)

Subject: Delays to the Luas light rail system for Dublin, Ireland

On 29 September the Irish Minister for Public Enterprise announced that a public inquiry on the Luas light rail system, a badly needed alternative to car use for Dublin's commuters, is to be adjourned for six months. This was called to allow further study on the option of running part of Luas underground, even though some studies have already deemed this option too costly.

Coras Iompar Éireann (CIE), the company behind the project, has said that there was a major risk to the project and that a major reappraisal of it would be likely if a decision was taken to run part of it underground.

When EU funding for the project was secured, the Commission stated that at least one of the three Luas lines, the one terminating at Dundrum, should be completed by 2001.

What view does the Commission take of the latest delays to the project's implementation?

Answer

The postponement of the public inquiry for six months will exacerbate the already difficult timing problems in relation to this project. In the light of this, the Monitoring Committee for the Community Support Framework (CSF) for Ireland which oversees all expenditure across the whole structural funds spectrum decided in July 1997 that ‚if in the spring of 1998 it cannot be proven that sufficient progress has been achieved to secure the likely completion of the LUAS project within the existing agreed timescale, all or part of the funds will be decommitted and reallocated to other projects within the CSF’.

This commitment to review the project may mean that regardless of the outcome of the studies and indeed the deliberations of the public inquiry, a definite decision will have to be made by the CSF Monitoring Committee in the spring of 1998. At this stage the Commission would not wish to pre‐empt these agreed arrangements for review of the project.

Question No 76 by Concepció Ferrer (H−0737/97)

Subject: Marketing of Catalan pigmeat products

At the last July part‐session I asked the Commission about Portugal's infringement of EC legislation by imposing a unilateral ban on imports of pigmeat products processed in Cataluña.

The Commission undertook in its reply to inform me directly of the outcome of its investigations; does it not think that four months are long enough for it to have concluded these investigations and therefore to be in a position to take the appropriate decisions with regard to this repeated failure to comply with Community regulations which is causing such damage to the Catalan meat industry?

Answer

On 11 June 1997 the Commission asked Portugal for information on the specific measures taken against the trade of pigmeat products from Cataluña.

After a further request, the Commission received a reply on 24 September, where it has been confirmed that, in order to avoid the spread of classical swine fever present in some areas of Spain, measures have been adopted to prohibit the trade of pigmeat products from Cataluña unless they are heat‐treated.

The Commission discussed the matter with the Portuguese veterinary authorities during the Standing Veterinary Committee on 7‐8 October. The Portuguese authorities stated that they intend to keep these measures in force until they obtain guarantees from the competent Spanish authorities that stricter precautions have been taken in order to prevent the spread of classic swine fever through trade.

The measures unilaterally adopted by Portugal are not within the framework of Commission Decision 97/285/EC concerning certain protection measures relating to classic swine fever in Spain.

The Commission is therefore seriously considering opening an infringement procedure pursuant to Article 169 of the EC Treaty against the Portuguese Government.

Question No 77 by Antonios Trakatellis (H−0741/97)

Subject: Greek Community Support Framework: Thessaloniki Underground

The Greek Government has submitted two mutually incompatible requests to the Commission regarding the Thessaloniki Underground: one to DG XVI concerning the transfer of Dr 27 billion and the other to DG XV concerning the award of the contract to the second‐lowest bidder, after spending two and a half years engaged in negotiations with the lowest bidder.

Is the Greek Government legally entitled to submit a request to DG XVI concerning the transfer of appropriations, while a decision by DG XV is still pending, and what will be the consequences of withdrawing the Dr 27 billion? What is the Commission's position regarding the contradictory nature and legality of the Greek Government's actions regarding the Thessaloniki Underground?

Answer

The Commission can confirm that the Ministry for the National Economy has proposed to reduce Dr 27 billion, that is 63 % of the initial allocation of the credits allowed by the second Community Support Framework (CSF) for the building of the Thessalonika metro system.

Since the new procedure for award of contracts by the Ministry of Public Works is the subject of a complaint currently being investigated by the Commission and in consideration of the fact that this project is at risk of not being finished before the end of the prescribed CSF period, there is a certain logic in the proposal by the Greek Government.

Question No 78 by MaLou Lindholm (H−0742/97)

Subject: Equality in the EC Staff Regulations

On 2 October, an appeal was lodged with the Court of First Instance concerning discrimination against same‐sex couples in the Communities' Staff Regulations. On 30 September 1997, the Advocate‐General noted in his opinion in a case involving a similar problem (C−249/96) that discrimination against same‐sex partners (spouses) when granting allowances and other benefits to officials was incompatible with article 119 of the Treaty. The legal and ethical basis for amending the Staff Regulations may already be considered to exist, but it was reinforced when, in signing the Treaty of Amsterdam on 2 October 1997, the members of the Council supported the prohibition of discrimination, pursuant to article 6a. Will the Commission be submitting a proposal to the Council during the current Presidency for amendments to the Staff Regulations which takes into account Parliament's position so as to include a broad interpretation of the term “partnership’ as the basis for granting allowances and other benefits to officials and to incorporate the banning of discrimination on the grounds of gender, sexuality, age and disability?

Answer

With regard to the recognition of the status of “partner’ recorded in the Statute of the European Communities Civil Service, the Commission is in the process of appealing to the court of first instance against a decision of the AIPN of the Council, together with the Conclusions of the Advocate General in case number C249/96. It will not fail to take account of the evolution of Community justice in the interpretation of the statutory arrangements in question.

In addition it is currently making a detailed study of the evolution of this matter at the level of the Member States, in order to appreciate the positive legal measures which might justify a proposal by the Commission to the Council.

The Commission reminds the honourable Member that it recently decreed measures in favour of couples of civil servants and other unmarried agents in anticipation of a first recognition of cohabitation situations such as granting to cohabitees certain administrative, non‐statutory and neutral facilities on the financial level

Question No 79 by David Martin (H−0746/97)

Subject: Impact on EU importers of the withdrawal of Bangladesh textile certificates of origin

As a result of a Commission investigation the Bangladesh Government has invalidated 7, 000 textile origin certificates.

While the Commission is to be applauded for its anti‐fraud activity does it believe that it is right that European importers who acted in good faith should be punished, through the collection of unpaid duties, for the errors of the Bangladeshi authorities?

Question No 80 by Alex Smith (H−0759/97)

Subject: The invalidation of GSP certificates from Bangladesh

The invalidation of GSP textile import certificates from Bangladesh will have severe repercussions both for the EU importers and the future of GSP preferences as a development instrument. In the light of this it is incomprehensible that the Commission has so far failed to clarify the following: what is the total of certificates invalidated and will the Commission insist on further invalidations, if so, how many; when will the envisaged derogation for Bangladesh be in place; what proportion of the trade will this derogation cover?

Would the Commission provide clarification on the above issues and would the Commission accept that the lack of transparency in its handling of the Bangladesh case seriously undermines the future confidence of EU importers in sourcing from countries which benefit from GSP preferences? Would the Commission agree that there is an urgent need for a clear solution and a harmonized approach in how to deal with the question of retroactive duties in cases where the EU importers have acted in good faith? Until such a solution has been found would the Commission support the view that the back duties to be paid by importers in the Bangladesh case should be waived?

Joint answer

1. In its Communication to the Council on the management of preferential tariff arrangements (COM(97)0402 final, 23 July 1997), the Commission outlines various discrepancies in the implementation of these arrangements, pointing out the adverse consequences both for Community traders and the beneficiary countries. In particular, it notes that the developing countries enjoy none or only some of the benefits inherent in the preferential tariff arrangements granted to them and that certain Community traders are forced to bear the consequences.

In the light of the situation, the Commission puts forward a series of proposals designed to remedy it.

2. In order to help Bangladesh establish a sustainable infrastructure and enable it to benefit fully from the advantages offered to it by the Community, a regulation providing for a temporary derogation from the rules governing origin is about to be adopted by the Commission. Its implementation date has been set for 15 October 1997.

3. That derogation covers roughly half of Bangladesh"s exports to the Community on the basis of a three‐year average. However, exports from Bangladesh which comply with the Community rules governing origin will continue as in the past and in addition to those covered by the ‚derogation" regulations. On the basis of talks with the Bangladeshi authorities, it would appear that the volume of exports which comply with the rules governing origin could quickly reach 20 % of the exports observed in the past.

4. The total of past exports for which statistics have been drawn up by the Community also includes fraudulent exports involving goods which did not even pass through Bangladesh. As a result, everything points to the fact that the volume of genuine exports from Bangladesh will be maintained at or a little below past levels.

5. The Bangladeshi authorities have acknowledged their responsibility by cancelling certificates of origin which are not consistent with the relevant rules governing origin. On 1 October 1997 Bangladesh withdrew more than 15, 000 certificates wrongly issued between 1994 and 1996. A communication seeking to clarify the situation was forwarded to the Member States through the usual channels on 2 October 1997.

6. As regards the responsibility of Community economic operators, the Commission shares the view expressed on a number of occasions by the Court of Justice, to the effects that the discovery after the import of goods that the certificate were wrongly issued represents a normal commercial risk and is consistent with general principles(8) . On that basis, no retroactive measures can be envisaged.

The SPG tariff preference represents one component of the cost of the goods concerned on the Community market and it is thus incumbent on any prudent importer to check that the goods covered by a commercial contract are eligible for preferential tariff arrangements.

7. In the Commission"s view, the political courage displayed by Bangladesh in withdrawing the illegally issued certificates offers Community economic operators an important guarantee that the country will henceforth apply the Community rules correctly.

Question No 81 by Niall Andrews (H−0750/97)

Subject: Mir space station

Can the Commission indicate the total amount of European funding committed to the Mir space station and can it also indicate if it is satisfied that European part‐funding of the Mir space station represents good value for money for European taxpayers?

Answer

No funding from the Community budget has ever been committed to the MIR Space Station.

At the level of the European Space Agency, Europe organized two EURO‐MIR missions in 1994 and 1995 in which a European astronaut was sent to MIR for a period of time of respectively 31 and 179 days. ESA's total payment for these missions was of the order of ECU 50 million including access to scientific experiment facilities in the Space Station.

It is difficult to compare the value of ECU 50 million with the value of seven months" human presence in space and the related scientific experimentation. Nevertheless it is beyond any doubt that the real cost of a man‐hour in space is much higher than what ESA paid and, over and above this, Europe itself does not have a manned space capability.

Question No 82 by Gerard Collins (H−0752/97)

Subject: Protectionist proposals by US for aircraft servicing

Will the Commission outline as a matter of urgency what action it is taking in response to the complaints made by the Association of European Airlines that proposed US legislation would limit the number of foreign companies allowed to service US aircraft, the comments that the legislation would change current regulations so that US aircraft are repaired to the maximum extent possible by professional US mechanics, properly trained and supervised, using certified parts, the inference being that European mechanics are neither professional nor properly trained, and does it realize that such legislation, if enacted, would be protectionist and could pose serious problems, for example, for Shannon in Ireland which has an experienced, professional and highly trained workforce engaged in the aircraft servicing business?

Answer

The Commission has received a letter from AEA concerning a hearing held on 9 October of the Aviation Subcommittee of the US Congress on a draft bill concerning foreign repair stations.

The Commission is monitoring the situation closely since such a bill, if enacted, could clearly have detrimental effects on European companies.

It is worth noting, of course, that the USA has included the foreign repair stations fully in its commitments under the General Agreement on Trade in Services (GATS). The US Government regard such commitments to be serious and the Commission is therefore confident that the USA will act in accordance with its international obligations.

Question No 83 by Pat Gallagher (H−0753/97)

Subject: Tourism initiatives and Agenda 2000

The tourism sector has the capacity to generate increased job opportunities, particularly in the peripheral regions and countries of the Union, provided that adequate levels of investment are allocated towards developing the sector's full potential.

In the context of the debate on Agenda 2000 and the future priorities for funding from the annual EU budget, can the Commission indicate if it intends to bring forward structural fund initiatives to continue funding growth and development in the tourism sector, particularly in peripheral areas?

Answer

Agenda 2000 establishes a financial framework for Community policies for the period 2000‐2006. The section on future structural policies at European level sets out the Commission's proposals for the total volume of spending over the period and the priority objectives under the structural funds.

The detail has yet to be worked out in the revised structural funds regulations which will be proposed by the Commission early next year. Agenda 2000 stresses, however, the need to raise competitiveness in the leastdeveloped areas and to promote diversification in areas dependent on declining activities. In addition, the Commission proposes a more decentralized system so that the priorities voiced by the assisted regions themselves can be taken into account. The extent of future support for measures in the tourism sector can be expected to be examined within this broad framework.

Question No 84 by Karin Riis‐Jørgensen (H−0765/97)

Subject: Free movement of undertakings in the EU

A Danish company, Easy‐Line, wishes to run a service between Rødby and Puttgarden, but has been denied access to the port areas on the German side. Moreover, Deutsche Bahn, which owns the areas, has informed the company that it will not be considering its application before December.

This is a matter of principle on several grounds, including competition and the free movement of undertakings within the EU.

Will the Commission state how it intends to ensure that State‐owned bodies in the individual Member States comply with the EU's competition rules, with specific reference to this case in which a Danish company is being denied access to a German port?

Answer

The Commission is grateful to the honourable Member for drawing to its attention the difficulties being faced by the Danish company Easy‐Line which wishes to operate a service on the Rødby‐Puttgarden route between Denmark and Germany. On that important route there is currently one ferry service, jointly operated by DFO, a subsidiary of DB, and DSB Rederi.

EC competition rules require that an undertaking that owns an essential port facility from which it provides a ferry service may not, without objective justification, refuse to grant access to a ferry operator which wishes to operate on the same route. Any such refusal would be an abuse of dominant position contrary to Article 86 of the EC Treaty. Those principles apply irrespective of whether the owner of the essential port facility is a public or private undertaking.

In 1993 the Commission applied those principles to a refusal to allow a competitor access to the Port of Rødby in order to operate on the Rødby‐Puttgarden route. In its decision, the Commission found the Rødby‐Puttgarden route to be an important link that faced only limited competition from other routes. The Commission decided that a competitor should be allowed either to use the port of Rødby or to build its own facilities near to Rødby.

If Easy‐Line continues to face difficulties, it is open to it to make a complaint to the national competition authorities or to the Commission. If the Commission were to receive a complaint, it would of course make enquiries and if necessary take measures to ensure compliance with the EC competition rules.

Question No 85 by Wolfgang Kreissl‐Dörfler (H−0768/97)

Subject: Human rights in Colombia

During his visit to Colombia this September, Commission Vice‐President Manuel Marín declared that 'without peace no human rights policy is possible in Colombia: it is a sine qua non'. He also voiced the EU's wish to help in the peace process.

If no peace process gets under way in Colombia before the present government's mandate expires, what form will the Commission's continuing collaboration take with regard to the task of overcoming the huge human rights problems afflicting the country? More specifically, what political and financial support will be forthcoming to maintain the Permanent Office of the High Commission in Colombia, whose mandate is fundamentally based on international human rights law?

Answer

1. As indicated by the honourable Member the Commission is following developments in Colombia with close concern. During the last visit to Colombia on 8 September, the Commissioner in charge of foreign relations with the countries of Latin America reiterated that the international community shares the desire of the Colombian people to find a peaceful solution to one of the most ancient internal conflicts in a Latin American country and that there is no alternative to the path of dialogue and national reconciliation.

As far as the peace initiatives are concerned the Commission noted with great interest the various steps taken by the Colombian Government, the Church and the non‐governmental organizations in the search for a dialogue between the various parties to the armed conflict, with a view to reaching a negotiated solution.

The scope for intervention by the Commission to aid these initiatives is nevertheless severely limited by the context of violence which prevails in this country, and by great general fluidity in the political situation. As a consequence, at the present time, action is being concentrated on humanitarian initiatives. The Commission is nevertheless ready to respond positively to any appropriate request which might facilitate development of the aforementioned peace process.

2. On the eve of the municipal elections of 26 October, the Commission is deeply concerned about the threats made by the guerillas and paramilitary groups against candidates in the elections and the assassination of some of these candidates. This situation imperils the democratic process which is in progress. Consequently the Commission recalls the appeal by the European Union to all the parties concerned that they respect both the democratic process and the desire of Colombians to exercise freely their right to vote.

3. Reinforcement of the state of law is one of the Commission"s action priorities in Colombia. It is making a significant contribution in this sphere by means of three main channels:

‐ reinforcement of the presence of the international community in the country, with the financing of five international observers from the office of the UN High Commissioner for Human Rights;

‐ financing of initiatives for local NGOs active in the domain of democracy and human rights;

‐ structural aid (capacity building) in the sector of the administration most directly responsible for the state of law. The Commission is currently preparing an important support project in the field of the administration of justice.

In addition the Commission is ready to continue to collaborate with the embassies of Member States of the Union in its visits to the regions of the country where the most serious human rights problems exist in order to monitor developments on the spot and identify any possible spheres in which the EU might give its support.

Question No 86 by Gianfranco Dell'Alba (H−0637/97/rev. 1)

Subject: Compatibility of the office of judge at the Court of Justice with the possibility of imminent legal proceedings

A former minister and politician, now a judge at the Court of Justice, has come under suspicion in numerous judicial inquiries under way in his home country. Although no formal proceedings have yet been instituted against him, further developments cannot be ruled out at this stage. The Commission performs a decisive role as guardian of the treaties in accordance with article 155 of the EC Treaty. Moreover, it is absolutely essential to preserve the independence and autonomy of the Court of Justice and avert the risk that it might be implicated, even indirectly, in situations that could undermine its credibility within the EU.

Going beyond the specific case referred to above, what attitude would the Commission take if, in the light of a judicial investigation in his own country, a member of the Court of Justice were suspected of committing a crime predating his appointment as a Community judge?

Answer

Pursuant to article 167 of the EC Treaty, judges of the Court of Justice are appointed by mutual agreement between the governments of Member States.

Under article 3 of the rules of the Court of Justice, judges benefit from immunity in legal proceedings. Only the court itself can withdraw that immunity.

Consequently, the Commission has no power to act as the honourable Member suggests.

Question No 87 by Bernd Posselt (H−0652/97)

Subject: Population policy for developing countries

The Commission will be spending ECU 35 million in support of population policies and programmes in the developing countries from 1998 to 2002. How will it ensure that the decision taken at the UN conference held in Cairo in 1994 forbidding the use of abortion as a means of birth control is not infringed and that programmes providing for abortion are not therefore assisted from EU resources?

Answer

The new Council Regulation of 22 July 1997 on ‚Aid for Population Policies and Programmes in Developing Countries’ which is the legal basis for the special budget line – B7‐631 – states in its preamble (paragraph 12) that ‚when cooperation measures are implemented abortion can never be promoted as a family planning method’. The Commission makes every reasonable effort to ensure that all projects comply with this statement, in particular through pre‐approval screening and/or through dialogue with the contracting partner. The Commission's position is, therefore, fully in line with the decision adopted at the 1994 International Conference on Population and Development in Cairo.

Ref: Council Regulation (EC) No 1484/97 of 22 July on aid for population policies and programmes in the developing countries. OJ No L 202/1‐30.7.97

Question No 88 by Birgitta Ahlqvist (H−0653/97)

Subject: Children in Belarus suffering the after‐effects of Chernobyl

When disaster struck at Chernobyl in 1986, the whole of the western world mobilized its resources. Now, however, it seems that the children who are still suffering from radiation‐related diseases such as thyroid cancer and leukemia have been forgotten. There is great need and destitution; children and young people are dying of diseases for which the prognosis in the EU would be good. A simple bout of influenza becomes a deadly threat when one's state of health is already so poor.

I am well aware of the hardening political climate in Belarus and, knowing the situation, it is shameful that we in the west have done so little in the eleven years that have passed since the Chernobyl disaster. What measures does the Commission intend to take to remedy this situation?

Answer

The Commission is tackling the problem of the secondary effects of the Chernobyl accident for some years, using different instruments for this purpose.

Tacis has provided assistance for health problems related to Chernobyl, in particular for the analysis and treatment of thyroid cancer, under the Chernobyl Regional Programming benefiting Russia, Ukraine and Belarus.

More substantially, the aid of the Commission through its European Communities Humanitarian Office, (ECHO), in favour of the victims of Chernobyl amounts to ECU 8.14 million. An important part of this budget is devoted to the treatment of children in Belarus, notably since 1994 through the provision of assistance to the Children Centre in Hospital No 1 set up in Minsk in 1991 to treat in particular thyroid cancer.

Although bilateral relations with Belarus have seriously deteriorated since last year, the Commission is still fully committed to continue providing assistance to the victims of Chernobyl, the children in particular.

The Commission approved on 9 September 1997 another ECU 1 million grant in aid for these victims. The aid will enable the Red Cross International Federation and Crescent Societies (IFRC) and two European non‐governmental organizations to carry out three programmes: ECU 575, 000 will enable the children surgical centre of Minsk Clinic No 1 to operate until the end of 1998; ECU 350, 000 is for the screening of Chernobyl‐related diseases in the areas of Belarus, Ukraine and Russia worst affected by the nuclear disaster; ECU 75, 000 will go towards supporting the main hospital treating thyroid cancers in Ukraine, the Kiev Institute of Endocrinology and Metabolism.

The Tacis Lien Programme, supporting the action of local NGOs for the weakest strata of the population, continues to operate in Belarus and is instrumental in assisting the Chernobyl children.

The activities of local NGOs which receive grants from foreign funders, like the Children of Chernobyl, are rendered more and more difficult as they are being harassed, subject to special inspections and heavy taxes. A recent regulation in imports of goods impedes the normal transportation of humanitarian aid (e.g. medical equipment) into the country, putting further financial strain on local partner NGOs.

Question No 89 by David Bowe (H−0664/97)

Subject: Shipments of waste to certain non‐OECD countries

With regard to the proposal for a Council regulation, establishing common rules and procedures to apply to shipments to certain non‐OECD countries of certain types of wastes (COM(94)0678), does the Commission think that its letters to all countries form the period 1993‐94, asking if they wanted to accept non‐hazardous waste for recycling, were sufficiently comprehensible to enable a rapid and complete response. Can the Commission say what language the letters were written in, how many pages they comprised, and how many countries have replied saying that they do not wish to receive any waste, but have nevertheless continued to permit the import of materials on the Basel Convention's 'green list' of wastes?

Answer

The Commission's letters in French and English (and later Spanish) were composed of twelve, mainly Yes/No questions, six of which asked the country in question if it wished to ship waste to the Community for environmentally sound recycling or disposal if facilities were not available in their own country. The draft letters had been submitted to the Member States for approval prior to sending them. A copy of Regulation 259/93 was attached to the letter. The honourable Member should be by now in possession of the package of documents sent to the countries concerned. Most of the replies were returned to the Commission within a year.

Forty‐six countries have now replied saying that they do not wish to receive waste and 54 have not yet replied.

From trade sources it is clear that shipments of non‐hazardous wastes, particularly metals, glass, paper and used clothing continue to be exported to many of these countries. Until the proposed regulation comes into force, the Commission can only inform exporters of the contents of the replies made in the ‚Note Verbale’ form.

The ‚green’ list of waste, that is to say the non‐hazardous waste list, originates from the OECD control procedure which are traded within the OECD system as ‚normal commercial transactions’. These wastes are considered by the OECD not to exhibit hazardous characteristics.

The Basel Convention deals only with the control of transboundary movements of hazardous wastes and their disposal and is not applicable to the current proposal.
